For example, there is a strong bias toward glycolysis over mitochondrial metabolism by proinflammatory CD4<sup>+</sup> Th1, Th2, and Th17 lineages whereas induced CD4<sup>+</sup> Treg lineage cells display a mixed metabolism involving glycolysis, lipid oxidation, and OXPHOS [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR18" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR18">18</a></span>]. It is noteworthy that blockade of glycolysis during in vitro Th17 differentiation favors Treg formation rather than Th17 cells [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR161" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR161">161</a></span>]. Addition of exogenous fatty acids (FAs) in the culture of T cells activated under skewing conditions strongly inhibits the production of Th1, Th2, and Th17 cytokines, but not the Treg suppressive function. Importantly, inhibition of Teff function in the presence of FAs cannot be rescued by re-addition of Th1-, Th2-, and Th17-promoting cytokines [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR18" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR18">18</a></span>].</p></div></section>
            <section xmlns="" xmlns:fn="http://www.w3.org/2005/xpath-functions" xmlns:meta="http://www.springer.com/app/meta" id="Sec19" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading js-ToggleCollapseSection">Effects of the tumor microenvironment on T cell fitness</h2><div class="js-CollapseSection"><section id="Sec20" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2">
                <h3 class="Heading">Exhaustion</h3>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par66" class="Para">T cell metabolic fitness is central to effective antitumor immunity but is compromised by unique conditions of limited nutrient availability in the tumor nutrient microenvironment and the effects of immune checkpoints [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR162" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR162">162</a></span>]. Within the immune suppressive tumor microenvironment, T cells acquire an exhausted phenotype, which is characterized by progressive loss of effector functions, changes in expression and function of key transcription factors, and metabolic alterations. In addition exhausted T cells are characterized by a sustained upregulation and co-expression of multiple inhibitory receptors [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR163" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR163">163</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR164" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR164">164</a></span>]. The multiple metabolic constraints imposed by the cancer cell metabolism in the tumor microenvironment minimize the potential of T cells to mediate effector function. It is likely that nutrient availability, effects of inhibitory receptors and metabolic changes of the microenvironment drive T cell differentiation to the state of exhaustion. T cells receiving signals through the key inhibitory receptor PD-1 are switched to low glycolytic and low OXPHOS state with limited biosynthetic activity and low antioxidant reserves [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR15" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR15">15</a></span>]. It is likely that such changes in metabolic reprogramming drive T cell differentiation to the state of exhaustion. T cell exhaustion is an active process and can lead to measurable consequences on T cell function. For example, distinct subsets of exhausted T cells exist with different potentials for recovering function after blockade of the PD-1 pathway. Exhausted T cells with intermediate expression of PD-1 (PD-1<sup>int</sup>) can be reinvigorated by blockade of the PD-1 pathway, whereas those with high expression of PD-1 (PD-1<sup>high</sup> cells) cannot [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR163" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR163">163</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR164" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR164">164</a></span>]. These distinct subsets of exhausted T cells may also have different bioenergetic properties and differential capacity for mitochondrial biogenesis. It is tempting to speculate that the degree of exhaustion and the ability to reinvigorate exhausted T cells might depend on the reserve of lipids, which appear to be the only source of energy generation by FAO in T cells receiving PD-1 signals [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR15" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR15">15</a></span>]. It is also possible that blockade of the PD-1: PD-L1 pathway may lead to transient regeneration of Teff cells if the metabolic defects of exhausted tumor-specific T cells are not fully recovered. Thus, identification of the distinct bioenergetic profiles in exhausted T cell subsets might provide new tools to determine the level of T cell exhaustion and also identify novel targets to reverse exhaustion in addition to PD-1 blockade.</p>
              </section><section id="Sec21" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2">
                <h3 class="Heading">Nutrients</h3>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par67" class="Para">The main nutrients that support survival and growth of T lymphocytes are glucose, amino acids, and lipids, and their deprivation results in impaired T cell function. Depending on the type of nutrients present, T cells can acquire distinct differentiation program and functional fate [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR21" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR21">21</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR165" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR165">165</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR166" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR166">166</a></span>]. In the tumor microenvironment cancer and T cells develop metabolic competition for the utilization of available nutrients among which glucose has a central role. Cancer cells are addicted to a steady state glycolysis, whereas T cells depend on glucose during the process of antigen-mediated activation that leads to expansion and development of effector functions. Limiting glucose in the culture decreases the activation of nave T cells and the production of effector cytokines although a significant degree of proliferation might be preserved through OXPHOS [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR19" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR19">19</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR148" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR148">148</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR167" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR167">167</a></span><span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR172" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR172">172</a></span>]. Mechanistically, glycolysis might promote IFN- production by inducing GAPDH activation and releasing GAPDH from binding at the 3UTR of the IFN- promoter, thereby inducing IFN- production via post- transciptional mechanisms [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR19" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR19">19</a></span>]. In addition, the glycolytic metabolite phosphoenolpyruvate (PEP) promotes T cell activation by sustaining T cell receptor-mediated Ca<sup>2+</sup>-NFAT signaling and effector functions due to repressing sarco/ER Ca<sup>2+</sup>-ATPase (SERCA) activity [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR17" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR17">17</a></span>]. Because glycolysis is intimately linked with T effector differentiation, glucose deprivation due to high glucose flux in the cancer cells results in limited glucose availability for utilization by T cells. As a consequence, T cells not only are unable to develop tumoricidal effector functions but also alter their differentiation program resulting in the generation of cell types that develop during limited glucose supplies, such as Treg and T exhausted T cells [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR15" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR15">15</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR18" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR18">18</a></span>].</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par68" class="Para">T cells also activate glutamine transport and catabolism mechanisms during their stimulation [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR167" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR167">167</a></span><span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR169" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR169">169</a></span>]. Glutamine has an essential role to support the TCA cycle through anaplerosis after prior conversion to glutamate and aKG and is also critical for ATP production via OXPHOS even in presence of glucose [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR20" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR20">20</a></span>]. Glutamate also serves as a key component for glutathione (GSH) synthesis and antioxidant defense [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR173" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR173">173</a></span>]. Similarly to glucose, cancer cells use glutamine for their metabolic needs [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR174" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR174">174</a></span>] indicating that competition for glutamine utilization by cancer and T cells also occurs in the tumor microenvironment. Therapeutic targeting of glutamine metabolism by glutaminase inhibitors in cancer cells is currently under investigation in clinical trials for hematologic malignancies and solid tumors (<span class="ExternalRef"><a href="https://www.clinicaltrials.gov/"><span class="RefSource">https://www.clinicaltrials.gov/</span></a></span>). It remains to be determined how such therapeutic approaches will affect T cell differentiation and function.</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par69" class="Para">Decreased levels of the essential amino acid, <span xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeSmallCaps">l</span>-tryptophan, can be caused by overexpression of IDO on tumor cells [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR175" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR175">175</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR176" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR176">176</a></span>]. It is well documented from studies in mouse models and patients that IDO expressed in cancer cells or DC in the cancer microenvironment mediates immunosuppressive effects and tolerance to tumor via mechanisms independent from <span xmlns="" class="EmphasisTypeSmallCaps">l</span>-tryptophan deprivation [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR177" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR177">177</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR178" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR178">178</a></span>]. Detailed recent studies designed to delineate the mechanisms of tumor cell IDO-induced immunosuppression showed that the effect of IDO in the tumor microenvironment is mediated through increased differentiation of Treg cells at the tumor site, systemic expansion of myeloid cells and marked recruitment of myeloid-derived suppressor cells (MDSCs) into the tumor microenvironment [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR179" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR179">179</a></span>]. Such correlation between IDO expression and MDSC infiltration has been identified not only in experimental animal models but also in samples from human patients with melanoma [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR179" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR179">179</a></span>]. Metabolic competition and nutrient deprivation, accumulation of metabolic byproducts, effects of IDO act in concert with microenvironmental changes induced by hypoxia and tumor aerobic glycolysis to form a barrier to antitumor T cell immunity.</p>
              </section><section id="Sec22" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2">
                <h3 class="Heading">Lactate</h3>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par70" class="Para">Lactic acid is largely produced by highly glycolytic cancer cells and can suppress the proliferation and cytokine production of human cytotoxic T lymphocytes (CTLs) and reduce cytotoxic activity [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR180" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR180">180</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR181" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR181">181</a></span>]. Activated T cells also secrete lactate but, due to their higher metabolic rate, cancer cells are most likely the key source of lactate in the tumor microenvironment. Moreover, since PD-1 ligation results in suppression of glycolysis and diminished lactate production [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR15" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR15">15</a></span>], engagement of PD-1 in T cells, in the context of cancer, will mitigate glycolysis and production of lactate. Thus, the main source of lactate is the tumor, and eliminating lactate production by tumor cells would be anticipated to improve T cell mediated antitumor immunity. A major consequence of lactate secretion is microenvironmental acidification. Studies that have addressed the role of acidic pH on immune function in the context of tumor have mostly focused on the impact of lactate-mediated acidification on macrophage polarization to the M2 suppressive phenotype [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR182" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR182">182</a></span>]. However, lactate-mediated acidification can also induce Arginase 1, which promotes the depletion of extracellular arginine levels, resulting in inhibition of efficient T cell proliferation and activation [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR182" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR182">182</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR183" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR183">183</a></span>]. Moreover, manipulation of the pH of the tumor microenvironment by the use of proton pump inhibitors results in less dysfunctional tumor infiltrating lymphocytes and increased therapeutic efficacy of both active and adoptive immunotherapy [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR184" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR184">184</a></span>]. Despite its negative impacts on the immune cells, certain cancer cell types can utilize lactate itself as a metabolic fuel through reverse conversion to pyruvate that is subsequently oxidized to provide a fuel in times of nutrient depletion [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR136" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR136">136</a></span>]. The extent to which T cells can oxidize lactate is unknown. It is also unknown whether such biochemical pathway might impact selectively on Teff, Tm, or Treg differentiation and/or function.</p>
              </section><section id="Sec23" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2">
                <h3 class="Heading">Hypoxia</h3>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par71" class="Para">Hypoxia occurs physiologically in the microenvironment of primary lymphoid organs, bone marrow, and thymus [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR185" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR185">185</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR186" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR186">186</a></span>] and plays a critical role in immune cell function and development [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR187" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR187">187</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR188" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR188">188</a></span>]. However, most prevalently, hypoxia is the hallmark of tissue microenvironments under pathological conditions as in the cases of cancer, inflammation, infection, necrosis, and autoimmunity. Under the survival- and growth-unfavorable conditions of the hypoxic microenvironment, all cells, including T lymphocytes, need to adapt metabolic programs that support their survival [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR148" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR148">148</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR189" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR189">189</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR190" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR190">190</a></span>]. Hypoxia inducible factors (HIFs) are the main transcription factors that sense and respond to hypoxia with HIF-1 and HIF-2 being the most widely studied members [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR191" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR191">191</a></span>]. The activity of HIFs is regulated by post-transcriptional modifications, which involve the hydroxylation of their proline and asparagine residues prolyl hydroxylases [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR192" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR192">192</a></span>]. The von HippelLindau tumor suppressor (VHL) protein binds to hydroxylated HIF1 targeting it for ubiquitination and degradation by the proteasome. In normoxic conditions, hydroxylation and proteasomal degradation of HIF1 subunits take place, whereas under hypoxic conditions the hydroxylases are no longer active, allowing HIF1 to translocate to the nucleus and bind to HIF1 in order to regulate the transcription [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR193" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR193">193</a></span>].</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par72" class="Para">The hypoxia response, which is based on the function of the HIF1 and HIF2 transcription factors, upregulates glucose transporters and multiple enzymes of the glycolytic pathway [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR128" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR128">128</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR194" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR194">194</a></span>]. In contrast, HIFs negatively regulate TCA and OXPHOS regulatory genes. In renal carcinoma cells lacking VHL protein, the main negative regulator of HIFs, HIF1 negatively regulates mitochondrial biogenesis and oxygen consumption by inhibiting the activity of c-Myc [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR195" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR195">195</a></span>]. Similarly to its effect in tumor cells, HIF1 mediates the metabolic switch from OXPHOS to aerobic glycolysis in lymphocytes [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR161" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR161">161</a></span>] and directly regulates the Th17/Treg balance in favor of Th17 cells [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR196" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR196">196</a></span>]. HIF1-deficient T cells display a marked reduction in the expression of IL-17 and Th17-signature genes. Moreover, mice with HIF1-deficient T cells are resistant to induction of Th17-dependent experimental autoimmune encephalitis, and this outcome is associated with diminished Th17 and increased Treg differentiation [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR196" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR196">196</a></span>]. HIF1 activates Th17 development through RORt and p300, and also attenuates Treg development by targeting Foxp3 for proteasomal degradation [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR161" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR161">161</a></span>]. As a consequence, blocking glycolysis during Th17 cell differentiation reduces the development of Th17 cells and favors Treg formation [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR161" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR161">161</a></span>], suggesting a direct link between metabolism and T cell fate determination. Conversely, deletion of VHL, which leads to elevated levels of HIFs, results in differentiation of cytotoxic CD8<sup>+</sup> lymphocytes (CTLs) with a high glycolytic and low OXPHOS activity and, thus, favors the differentiation of effector CD8<sup>+</sup> T cells [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR197" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR197">197</a></span>]. It is a subject of active investigation how HIF1 affects memory formation and anti-tumor immunity. Importantly, loss of HIF1 alters expression of chemokines and receptors involved in migration and extravasation. Loss of HIF1 results in sustained CD62L expression and increased of T cell homing to secondary lymphoid organs. In addition, loss of HIF1 results in upregulation of CXCR3, CCR5, and CCR7 in CD8<sup>+</sup> T cells [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR198" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR198">198</a></span>]. Moreover, hypoxia may protect tumor cells from antitumor immunity and can promote HIF1-dependent transcriptional upregulation of PD-L1 on cancer cells and myeloid-derived suppressor cells (MDSCs) that may inhibit PD-1-expressing T cells [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR199" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR199">199</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR200" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR200">200</a></span>]. Indeed, tumor cells have greater resistance to T cell-mediated killing under hypoxic conditions [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR200" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR200">200</a></span>]. Thus, hypoxia affects both tumor and T cells and shapes the expression of critical transcription factors, effector molecules, chemokines, chemokine receptors, costimulatory receptors, and activation-induced inhibitory receptors in a HIF-dependent manner. Therapeutic strategies targeting HIFs in the immune system might be beneficial for anti-tumor immunity.</p>
              </section><section id="Sec24" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2">
                <h3 class="Heading">Reactive oxygen species</h3>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par73" class="Para">Besides being considered as harmful by-products of metabolism or weapons of phagocytes against pathogens, ROS can also serve as signaling messengers in a multitude of pathways. It has also become evident that the source, kinetics and localization of ROS production all influence cell responses [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR201" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR201">201</a></span><span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR204" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR204">204</a></span>]. Tumors are influenced by changes in ROS production, and previous reports actually indicate that cancer cells exhibit more oxidative stress than their healthy counterparts [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR205" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR205">205</a></span>]. In particular, mitochondria-derived ROS are essential for Kras-mediated cancer cell growth [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR5" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR5">5</a></span>]. In contrast, other studies showed that inhibition or abrogation of LDH-A causes a shift to higher OCR and ROS production from the mitochondria that render the tumor cells more susceptible to apoptosis [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR206" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR206">206</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR207" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR207">207</a></span>]. Moreover, it has been shown that increase in intracellular concentrations of ROS causes inhibition of the glycolytic enzyme PKM2 leading to glucose diversion into the PPP, thereby generating sufficient reducing power for detoxification of ROS [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR208" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR208">208</a></span>]. In a recent article by James Watson, co-discoverer of the double helix structure of DNA, it was pointed out that causing oxidative stress by targeting highly expressed antioxidant enzymes, which are not present in non-malignant cells, holds great promise as a strategy for finding novel anti-tumor drugs. In contrast, antioxidants seem to promote tumor survival and growth [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR209" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR209">209</a></span>]. Interestingly, cultured colorectal cancer cells (CRC) harboring KRAS or BRAF mutations, which upregulate the glucose transporter Glut1, are selectively killed when exposed to high levels of vitamin C, but this is not due to the antioxidant function of vitamin C. Instead, this effect is mediated by the increased uptake of the oxidized form of vitamin C, dehydroascorbate (DHA) via the Glut1 glucose transporter, which is subsequently reduced to vitamin C, thereby depleting glutathione and causing oxidative stress. Thus, in cancer cells ROS accumulates and inactivates GAPDH leading to an energetic crisis and cell death not seen in CRC cells not harboring KRAS and BRAF mutations [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR210" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR210">210</a></span>].</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par74" class="Para">In T cells, ROS can be derived from the catalytic activity of NADPH oxidase 2 (NOX-2), which is a catalytic subunit of phagocyte oxidase (PHOX) expressed in the plasma membrane of T cells, or from dual oxidase I (DUOX-1), which is a cytoplasmic non-phagocytic isoform of NADPH oxidase [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR204" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR204">204</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR211" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR211">211</a></span>]. ROS can be generated from the electron transport chain of mitochondria [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR212" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR212">212</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR213" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR213">213</a></span>] as well as by lipoxygenases [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR214" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR214">214</a></span>]. T cell activation is paralleled by transient generation of low, physiologically relevant levels of ROS, which facilitates activation of ROS-dependent transcription factors, NF-kB and AP-1 [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR215" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR215">215</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR216" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR216">216</a></span>]. This oxidative signal is indispensable for T cell activation. Together with a Ca<sup>2+</sup> influx, it constitutes the minimal requirement for activation-induced gene expression (e.g., interleukin 2 [IL-2], IL-4, CD95 ligand) [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR203" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR203">203</a></span>]. In contrast to the indispensable role of low ROS levels in T cell activation, prolonged exposure to high ROS concentrations can inhibit T cell proliferation and lead to apoptosis [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR217" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR217">217</a></span>]. In addition, incubation of T cells with reactive nitrogen species (RNS) such as peroxynitrite can inhibit proliferation [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR218" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR218">218</a></span>]. Oxidative stress-induced modification to selective molecules involved in T cell receptor (TCR) signaling can render T cells hyporesponsive to activating stimuli [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR219" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR219">219</a></span>]. The redox environment also affects T cell differentiation. Peripheral blood mononuclear cells (PBMC), stimulated with a ROS generator promoted Th2 and inhibited Th1 differentiation [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR220" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR220">220</a></span>]. Moreover, products of lipid peroxidation, such as 4-hydroxy-2-nonenal (4HNE) and malonyldialdehyde (MDA), promote differentiation towards a Th2 phenotype [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR221" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR221">221</a></span>]. Interestingly, NOX-2 deficiency leads to differentiation towards the Th17 lineage [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR222" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR222">222</a></span>]. Since ROS can affect critical metabolism-related T cell signaling pathways such as the MAPKs and Akt pathways [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR204" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR204">204</a></span>], it is not unexpected that ROS would directly affect T cell differentiation and function. A study of the novel mechanism of Treg-mediated suppression by extracellular redox remodeling showed that murine Tregs suppress GSH synthesis and cysteine release by DCs in a CTLA-4-dependent manner, leading to the oxidation of surface thiols, decrease in the major cellular antioxidant GSH, and reduced proliferation of conventional T cells [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR223" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR223">223</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR224" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR224">224</a></span>]. Vitamin C, a naturally occurring antioxidant, can facilitate demethylation of Foxp3 enhancer and thus promote Treg generation [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR225" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR225">225</a></span>].</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par75" class="Para">In a recent work from our group, metabolite analysis of T cells in the presence of the checkpoint inhibitor PD-1 showed that PD-1 ligation resulted in significantly more pronounced decrease in the levels of reduced GSH. However, T cells receiving PD-1 signals displayed higher levels of cysteine-GSH disulfide and ophthtalmate, a GSH-like product synthesized by the same enzymes. These changes indicate a higher attempt to increase GSH synthesis, which, together with the more pronounced decrease in the levels of reduced GSH, are suggestive of a more oxidative environment in T cells receiving PD-1 signals [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR15" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR15">15</a></span>]. Consistent with a role of PD-1 in generating a more oxidative environment, another study showed that, following allogeneic bone marrow transplantation, alloreactive T cells simultaneously upregulated PD-1 expression and ROS production derived by FAO, resulting in higher susceptibility to metabolic inhibition by F1F0-ATP synthase complex inhibitors, and this event could be reversed by antioxidants. In that context, PD-1-blockade decreased mitochondrial H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and total cellular ROS levels as well as the efficacy of ROS-dependent inhibitory modulation of F1F0-ATP synthase complex [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR226" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR226">226</a></span>]. Although high ROS is toxic for T cells as it induces oxidative metabolic damage to various biochemical substrates, it should be noted that moderate levels of ROS have an indispensable role on T cell activation. The important involvement of ROS to T cell metabolic fate and function was highlighted by the recent identification of lymphocyte expansion molecule (LEM). LEM has no effects on glycolysis but controls the levels of OXPHOS complexes and respiration, resulting in the production of pro-proliferative mitochondrial ROS, which is critical for promoting antigen-dependent CD8<sup>+</sup> T cell proliferation, effector function, and long-term protective memory cells in response to infection with lymphocytic choriomeningitis virus [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR227" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR227">227</a></span>].</p>
                <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par76" class="Para">Based on the above, it is not evident whether the use of antioxidants or oxidants would be a beneficial therapeutic approach in the context of cancer. Caution should be exercised since such interventions will, most likely, also modulate T cell responses in addition to targeting cancer cells. One potential approach would be to target antioxidant genes in cancer in order to suppress the antioxidant defense mechanisms and make them susceptible to ROS-mediated apoptosis. Considering the fact that ROS are beneficial for T cell activation, a fine balance should be achieved in order to maximize anti-tumor effects without compromising T cell function.</p>
                <section id="Sec25" class="Section3 RenderAsSection3">
                  <h4 class="Heading">Checkpoint inhibitors</h4>
                  <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par77" class="Para">During the past 5&nbsp;years, cancer immunotherapy based on therapeutic targeting of checkpoint pathways has become a field of broad interest. This approach is based on the properties of T cells, which require at least two signals for activation. The first signal is mediated through TCR by recognition of specific antigen presented by the major histocompatibility complex (MHC) on antigen presenting cells (APCs). The second signal is mediated through ligation of co-stimulatory and co-inhibitory receptors, which are engaged by specific ligands expressed on APCs. The key co-stimulatory and co-inhibitory receptor-ligand pairs belong to the B7 and tumor necrosis factor (TNF) families [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR228" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR228">228</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR229" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR229">229</a></span>] although accessory molecules belonging to different families might also be important. The discovery that cytotoxic T-lymphocyte-associated protein 4 (CTLA4)- and programmed cell death protein 1 (PD-1)-dependent mechanisms provide the basis for the physiologic peripheral immunological tolerance supported the rationale that blocking the inhibitory signals mediated by CTLA-4 or PD-1 would promote activation of T cells that can recognize tumor antigens and induce anti-tumor responses. Importantly, the ligands of CTLA-4 B7-1 (CD80) and B7-2 (CD86) are expressed on antigen presenting cells (APC) but not on cancer cells, whereas the ligands of PD-1, PD-L1 and PD-L2, are expressed on APC and on cancer cells [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR12" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR12">12</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR13" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR13">13</a></span>]. The properties of these inhibitory immune checkpoint pathways leading to cancer immune evasion fostered the establishment of novel cancer immunotherapies.</p>
                  <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par78" class="Para">Currently, there is only limited knowledge regarding the metabolic consequences induced in T cells and/or tumor cells by targeting these immune checkpoint pathways. Laboratory studies provide evidence that inhibitory checkpoint pathways can alter metabolic reprogramming of T cells [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR15" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR15">15</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR230" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR230">230</a></span>]. T cells receiving PD-1 signals are unable to engage in glycolysis, glutaminolysis, or amino acid metabolism but display an increased rate of FAO [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR15" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR15">15</a></span>]. This effect of PD-1 is due to inhibition of glucose and glutamine transport as well as inhibition of HK2, which catalyzes the first step of glycolysis. These studies also determined that PD-1 promotes FAO of endogenous lipids by inhibiting the lipid oxidation PI3K pathway, resulting in increased expression of the rate-limiting enzyme of mitochondrial FAO carnitine palmitoyltransferase 1A (CPT1A), which plays an important role in the utilization of fatty acids as an energy source [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR231" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR231">231</a></span>]. In addition, PD-1 induces lipolysis, as determined by the increase of the major triacylglycerol hydrolase desnutrin/adiposite triglyceride lipase (ATGL) and release of fatty acids and glycerol. Concomitantly, PD-1 decreases lipid biosynthesis, which normally occurs during T cell activation, by abrogating the induction of fatty acid synthase (FASN). Consistent with the increased rate of FAO, PD-1 induces a significant elevation of the ketone body 3-hydroxybutyrate, which is produced during FAO. Compared to T cells activated without PD-1 ligation, activated T cells receiving PD-1 signals have lower extracellular acidification rate (ECAR), an indicator of glycolysis, and lower oxygen consumption rate (OCR), an indicator of oxidative phosphorylation, but have higher OCR/ECAR ratio [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR15" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR15">15</a></span>]. These findings indicate that in contrast to proliferating T cells, which preferentially use glycolysis for energy production, T cells receiving PD-1 signals are rather metabolically quiescence and preferentially use oxidative phosphorylation over glycolysis as indicated by the higher OCR/ECAR ratio. The enhancement of FAO also points to a mechanistic explanation for the longevity of T cells receiving PD-1 signals in patients with chronic infections and cancer and for their capacity to be reinvigorated by PD-1 blockade. Thus, PD-1 ligation alters the metabolic reprogramming induced upon T cell activation by inhibiting glycolysis and promoting FAO. In contrast, although CTLA-4 inhibits expression of the glutamine and glucose transporters, it inhibits glycolysis without augmenting CPT1A and FAO, suggesting that CTLA-4 maintains immune quiescence by preserving the metabolic profile of non-stimulated cells. The role of PD-1 signaling in restraining T cell glucose metabolism in vivo is also supported by another study in which allogeneic PD-L1<sup>/</sup> bone marrow transplant recipients had elevated levels of Glut1 and lactate production [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR232" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR232">232</a></span>].</p>
                  <p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par79" class="Para">In addition to its effects on metabolic reprogramming of T cells, the PD-1: PD-L1 pathway may also have implications on the metabolism of cancer cells. It has been observed that subpopulations of established human and murine melanoma cell lines as well as subpopulations of malignant cells in melanomas from patients biopsies express PD-1 [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR233" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR233">233</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR234" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR234">234</a></span>]. Unlike T cells in which PD-1 ligation causes inhibition of PI3K/Akt and MAPK pathways, PD-1 ligation in melanoma cells was found to activate these pathways and to induce mTOR signaling. In this context, ligation of PD-1 with PDL-1 in melanoma cells augments the mTOR-signaling pathway and promotes the expression of glycolytic enzymes, which correlate with tumor growth. Thus, tumor cells may use this pathway not only to escape immune response by suppressing activation and expansion of tumor-infiltrating T cells but, potentially, to actively support their growth by triggering mTOR signaling in trans in neighboring tumor cells thereby creating an intra-tumoral growth signal. It has also been reported that PD-L1 expressed on tumor cells can activate glycolysis and cancer cell growth [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR16" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR16">16</a></span>]. Although it is unclear how PD-L1, which has a short cytoplasmic chain without an evident signaling motif, would be ligated under physiologic conditions to trigger tumor-specific activation of glycolytic metabolism, together these findings [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR16" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR16">16</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR234" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR234">234</a></span>] suggest that the PD-1: PD-L1 pathway may have significant implications not only on T cell anabolic metabolism but also on cancer cell metabolism and growth.</p>
                </section>
              </section></div></section>
            <section xmlns="" xmlns:fn="http://www.w3.org/2005/xpath-functions" xmlns:meta="http://www.springer.com/app/meta" id="Sec26" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading js-ToggleCollapseSection">Harnessing metabolism therapeutically against cancer</h2><div class="js-CollapseSection"><p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par80" class="Para">So far there has been an extensive characterization of the metabolic features and aberrations in cancer. Cancer cell growth and survival relies on altered metabolic pathways such as aerobic glycolysis, fatty acid synthesis, and glutamine metabolism. In addition, emerging evidence points to a link between resistance in cancer treatment and deregulated cancer metabolism. Targeting cancer metabolism has therefore emerged as a promising new strategy for the development of anticancer agents either used alone or in combination therapies. Such drugs target signaling mediators including enzymes and transcription factors linked to pathways involved in cancer metabolism [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR235" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR235">235</a></span>]. To date, only few of these drugs are FDA approved due to unwanted side effects, whereas many are in clinical testing or at pre-clinical stage of development [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR236" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR236">236</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR237" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR237">237</a></span>]. Examples of such drugs targeting the glycolytic pathway include inhibitors of GLUTs, HK, PKM2 or LDHA [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR235" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR235">235</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR236" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR236">236</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR238" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR238">238</a></span>]. Other drugs target the PPP through inhibition of G6P with the goal to inhibit generation of nucleotide precursors. A G6P inhibitor, 6-aminonictinamide (6-AN), has efficacy against cancer cells in vitro [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR239" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR239">239</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR240" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR240">240</a></span>]. Other inhibitors of the PPP pathway such as resveratrol and dehydroepiandrosterone (DHEA) have also shown promising in vitro anti-cancer effects [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR235" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR235">235</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR241" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR241">241</a></span>]. Compounds targeting lipid metabolism have also been tested as anti-cancer drugs with the goal to decrease energy generation through FAO or to limit precursors for synthesis of fatty acid, which are necessary for cancer cell proliferation. For example, metformin, the FDA-approved and widely used anti-diabetic drug inhibits acetyl-CoA carboxylase (ACC) through increased AMPK activation, and there are preclinical and clinical data suggesting its anticancer effects [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR236" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR236">236</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR242" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR242">242</a></span><span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR244" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR244">244</a></span>]. In addition, inhibitors of FASN such as the FDA-approved drug orlistat are under preclinical evaluation for cancer treatment [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR235" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR235">235</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR242" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR242">242</a></span>]. Furthermore, inhibitors of FAO, such as the CPT1 inhibitor ranolazine, an FDA-approved compound for the treatment of angina, have shown promising anti-cancer effects [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR235" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR235">235</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR242" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR242">242</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR245" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR245">245</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR246" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR246">246</a></span>]. Despite the beneficial effects on anti-tumor treatment of such metabolism-targeting drugs, potential effects on the immune system have not yet been examined. However, it should be noted that when used in vivo, such metabolism-targeting drugs might mediate unwanted side effects on healthy tissues and organs that depend on the metabolic pathways targeted. For example, preclinical studies with 6-AN showed severe cytotoxicity on neuronal and hematopoietic cells [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR239" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR239">239</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR240" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR240">240</a></span>].</p><p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par81" class="Para">After the clinical success of antibodies against checkpoint inhibitors in multiple types of cancer, the role of the adaptive immune system in fighting cancer has become unequivocal. As a consequence, every effort to develop anti-tumor drugs is now accompanied by tests of such drugs on immune cells with the goal to confirm that no unwanted immunosuppressive function that would compromise anti-tumor immunity will be induced. Ideally, anti-tumor drugs or combination therapies should prevent tumor growth but simultaneously favor prolonged anti-tumor T cell function so that functional anti-tumor T cells can develop and possibly synergize with the tumor-specific cytotoxic functions of the therapeutic compound. A major goal of novel immunomodulatory approaches is the generation of tumor-specific Tm in parallel to the generation of Teff cells. This will allow for sustained immune-mediated anti-tumor function instead of a transient anti-tumor effect. For example, metformin, an anti-diabetic drug that, as mentioned above, has also shown direct clinical efficacy in cancer [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR243" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR243">243</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR244" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR244">244</a></span>], is an AMPK activator. As a consequence, metformin inhibits mTOR and glycolysis, thereby inhibiting tumor growth. Importantly, metformin also inhibits glycolysis by mediating direct inhibitory effects on key components of the glycolytic pathway including the rate-limiting enzyme of glycolysis HK2 [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR247" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR247">247</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR248" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR248">248</a></span>]. However, via these mechanisms metformin also promotes development of Treg [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR18" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR18">18</a></span>] and long-lived Tm cells [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR154" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR154">154</a></span>]. In addition, mTOR inhibiting compounds such as rapamycin can also have metabolism-targeting effects on T cells, and although rapamycin is traditionally being used as immunosuppressant, it can promote memory CD8<sup>+</sup> T cell formation when administered after an acute viral or bacterial infection [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR81" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR81">81</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR154" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR154">154</a></span>, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR155" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR155">155</a></span>]. Thus the use of metabolism-targeting drugs together with checkpoint inhibitors might alter the activation and differentiation program of tumor-specific T cells and prevent the generation of exhausted T cells.</p></div></section>
            <section xmlns="" xmlns:fn="http://www.w3.org/2005/xpath-functions" xmlns:meta="http://www.springer.com/app/meta" id="Sec27" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading js-ToggleCollapseSection">Conclusions and future directions</h2><div class="js-CollapseSection"><p xmlns:func="http://oscar.fig.bmc.com" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="Par82" class="Para">T cells and cancer cells inexorably share metabolic programs and preferences, and thus there is high competition for nutrients between cancer and T cells within the tumor microenvironment. Nutrient deprivation, increased metabolic waste, and the ability of tumors to express inhibitory ligands impair the metabolic fitness and capacity of T cells to uptake and utilize nutrients. Additionally, metabolism determinants of the tumor microenvironment drive T cells to exhaustion and Treg differentiation programs rather than Teff and Tm phenotypes leading to impaired antitumor responses. The changes and adaptations in the tumor microenvironment most likely are not limited to solid tumors because leukemia and lymphoma cells have similar metabolic characteristics with solid tumors and often express immunomodulatory ligands [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR249" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR249">249</a></span><span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR252" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR252">252</a></span>]. In addition, lymphomas may also contain infiltrating T cells with an exhausted phenotype similar to that identified in chronic viral infections or solid tumors [<span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR249" data-toggle="citation" data-target="CR249">249</a></span>]. Thus, drugs that directly target key metabolic enzymes or their upstream regulators will likely interfere with metabolism of both cancer and T cells in which core cell signaling and metabolic pathways converge. Understanding the similarities and differences of metabolic vulnerabilities of T cells and cancer may lead to the development of single-target or combination-based therapies to modify metabolism of the tumor niche thereby targeting both cancer cells and immune cells. Identification of such specific changes in oncometabolites and immunometabolites may define not only novel therapeutic targets but also biomarkers for assessment of therapeutic responses to tumor-immunotherapy combined with metabolism-targeting drugs. The ultimate goal is to design metabolism-based treatment strategies to attack and eradicate cancer while promoting effective and sustainable anti-tumor T cell responses.</p></div></section>
